<div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Formula 1 Grand Prix</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Formula One race. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/German_Grand_Prix_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="German Grand Prix (disambiguation)">German Grand Prix (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Eifel_Grand_Prix" class="mw-redirect" title="Eifel Grand Prix">Eifel Grand Prix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg_Grand_Prix" title="Luxembourg Grand Prix">Luxembourg Grand Prix</a>, or <a href="/wiki/European_Grand_Prix" title="European Grand Prix">European Grand Prix</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>German Grand Prix</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Großer Preis von Deutschland</i>) was a motor race that was held most years since 1926, with 75 races having been held. The race has been held at only three venues throughout its history; the <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rhineland-Palatinate" title="Rhineland-Palatinate">Rhineland-Palatinate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hockenheimring" title="Hockenheimring">Hockenheimring</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a> and occasionally <a href="/wiki/AVUS" title="AVUS">AVUS</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>. The race continued to be known as the German Grand Prix, even through the era when the race was held in <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>.
</p><p>Because West Germany was prevented from taking part in international events in the immediate post-war period, the German Grand Prix only became part of the <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> World Championship in 1951. It was designated the <a href="/wiki/European_Grand_Prix" title="European Grand Prix">European Grand Prix</a> four times between 1954 and 1974, when this title was an honorary designation given each year to one Grand Prix race in Europe. It has been organised by the <a href="/wiki/Automobilclub_von_Deutschland" title="Automobilclub von Deutschland">Automobilclub von Deutschland</a> (AvD) since 1926.
</p><p>The German Grand Prix was held at <a href="/wiki/Hockenheimring" title="Hockenheimring">Hockenheimring</a> every year between 1977 and 2006 (except 1985). During this time, a separate F1 race was held in Germany at the <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> most years from <a href="/wiki/1995_Formula_One_World_Championship" title="1995 Formula One World Championship">1995</a> until 2007 under the title of the <a href="/wiki/European_Grand_Prix" title="European Grand Prix">European Grand Prix</a>. Originally intended to begin in 2007, <a href="/wiki/Hockenheimring" title="Hockenheimring">Hockenheimring</a> and the <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> alternated hosting the German Grand Prix between 2008 and 2014, at which point Nürburgring pulled out of hosting the event in 2015, leaving Hockenheim the sole host of the race but only in alternating years until 2018. A further one-year deal placed the German Grand Prix on the 2019 calendar. As of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, a race under the name of  'German Grand Prix' has not been run again although Germany hosted the <a href="/wiki/2020_Eifel_Grand_Prix" title="2020 Eifel Grand Prix">2020 Eifel Grand Prix</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1907, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> staged the first of the <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiserpreis" title="Kaiserpreis">Kaiserpreis</a></i> races at the 73-mile (118&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Taunus" title="Taunus">Taunus</a> public road circuit, just outside <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>. The same circuit had been used three years earlier for the <a href="/wiki/1904_Gordon_Bennett_Cup" title="1904 Gordon Bennett Cup">1904 Gordon Bennett Cup</a> race. Entries were limited to touring cars with engines of less than eight litres. The race itself was a tragedy; a driver and his co-driver were killed 19 miles into the lap and the Taunus circuit was never used again. There was a medical team there, but it took them two and a half hours to get to the site of the accident, of which driver <a href="/w/index.php?title=Otto_G%C3%B6bel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Otto Göbel (page does not exist)">Otto Göbel</a> was badly injured and his co-driver Ludwig Faber, who was pinned under their <i>Adler</i> was already dead. Göbel died of his injuries in hospital later on.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Italy's <a href="/wiki/Felice_Nazzaro" title="Felice Nazzaro">Felice Nazzaro</a> won the race in a <a href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat">Fiat</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prinz-Heinrich-Fahrt" title="Prinz-Heinrich-Fahrt">Prinz-Heinrich-Fahrt</a> was the biggest German international race which was held from 1908 to 1911 and was organized by Prince Albert Wilhelm Heinrich,  These races were point-to-point races which would last a week, would start in Berlin and the competitors had to traverse varying terrains over multiple countries covering around 2,000&#160;km (1,250&#160;mi). Although this kind of racing had been banned in France because of multiple fatal accidents, these races were held with touring cars and the first rallies of any kind and had equal prestige of the <i>Kaiserpreis</i> races before them with drivers such as Nazzaro competing in them.
</p><p>In the early 1920s, <a href="/wiki/ADAC" title="ADAC">ADAC</a> <a href="/wiki/Eifelrennen" title="Eifelrennen">Eifelrennen</a> races were held on the twisty 33.2&#160;km (20.6&#160;mi) <a href="/wiki/Nideggen" title="Nideggen">Nideggen</a> public road circuit near <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>. Sometime around 1925, the construction of a dedicated race track was proposed just south of the Nideggen circuit around the ancient castle of the town of <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburg" title="Nürburg">Nürburg</a>, following the examples of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Autodromo_Nazionale_Monza" class="mw-redirect" title="Autodromo Nazionale Monza">Monza</a> and Targa Florio courses, and <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/AVUS" title="AVUS">AVUS</a>, yet with a different character. The layout of the circuit in the mountains was similar to the <a href="/wiki/Targa_Florio" title="Targa Florio">Targa Florio</a> event, one of the most important motor races at that time. The original Nürburgring was to be a showcase for <a href="/wiki/German_automotive_engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="German automotive engineering">German automotive engineering</a> and racing talent. Construction of the track, designed by the <i>Eichler Architekturbüro</i> from <a href="/wiki/Ravensburg" title="Ravensburg">Ravensburg</a> (led by architect Gustav Eichler), began in September 1925.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-war">Pre-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="AVUS">AVUS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: AVUS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915,_Berlin,_AVUS,_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915%2C_Berlin%2C_AVUS%2C_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915%2C_Berlin%2C_AVUS%2C_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915%2C_Berlin%2C_AVUS%2C_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915%2C_Berlin%2C_AVUS%2C_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915%2C_Berlin%2C_AVUS%2C_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915%2C_Berlin%2C_AVUS%2C_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="731" data-file-height="478" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02915,_Berlin,_AVUS,_Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Deutschland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Caracciola" title="Rudolf Caracciola">Rudolf Caracciola</a> and his mechanic Eugen Salzer celebrate victory at the first ever German Grand Prix in <a href="/wiki/1926_German_Grand_Prix" title="1926 German Grand Prix">1926</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The first national event in German <a href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_motor_racing" title="Grand Prix motor racing">Grand Prix motor racing</a> was held at the <a href="/wiki/AVUS" title="AVUS">AVUS</a> (Automobil Verkehrs und Übungs-Straße (Automobile Traffic and Practice Road)) race circuit in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> in 1926 as a sports car race. The AVUS circuit was made up of two 6-mile straights combined with two left-hand hairpins at each end. The first race at AVUS, in heavy rain, was won by Germany's native son, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Caracciola" title="Rudolf Caracciola">Rudolf Caracciola</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_in_Formula_One" title="Mercedes-Benz in Formula One">Mercedes-Benz</a>. The event was marred by <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Rosenberger" title="Adolf Rosenberger">Adolf Rosenberger</a>'s crash into one of the marshals' huts, killing three people. The AVUS circuit was considered extremely dangerous even back then- so the event was moved.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The German <i>Grand Prix</i> became an official event in 1929. Although it was raced on in the non-championship AVUS-Rennen in the 1930s which saw some of the fastest road races ever held, the Grand Prix would not return to AVUS until 1959 for a one-off appearance.
</p>
<h4><span id="N.C3.BCrburgring"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nürburgring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Grand Prix</i> moved to the new, 28.3&#160;km (17.6&#160;mi) <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a>, located in the Eifel Mountain region in western Germany about 70 miles (112&#160;km) from <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>. It was inaugurated on 18 June 1927 with the annual race, the <a href="/wiki/ADAC" title="ADAC">ADAC</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Eifelrennen" title="Eifelrennen">Eifelrennen</a></i>. This was a huge, incredibly challenging racing circuit that sped and twisted through forests of the Eifel Mountains, and had over 1000 feet (300 m) of elevation change and many spots where the cars visibly left the ground, such as the Flugplatz, Brunnchen and Pflanzgarten sections. There were two more races on the <i>Gesamtstrecke</i> (Combined circuit) combined course, which were both sportscar races, where German pre-war great <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Caracciola" title="Rudolf Caracciola">Rudolf Caracciola</a> would win his second of six German Grands Prix.
</p><p>The 1930 and 1933 races were cancelled due to economic reasons related to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>. In 1931, the event began to use only the 14.2-mile (22.8&#160;km) <i>Nordschleife</i> (North Loop), and this would continue onwards throughout the century. Caracciola would win the 1931 and 1932 events in a Mercedes and an Alfa Romeo respectively. Starting in 1934, there were often several races each year with the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Silver_Arrows" title="Silver Arrows">Silver Arrows</a>" <i>Grand Prix</i> cars in Germany, e.g. the <i>Eifelrennen</i>, the AVUS race and several <a href="/wiki/Hillclimbing" title="Hillclimbing">hillclimbs</a>. Yet it was only the Grand Prix at the Nürburgring that was the national <i><a href="/wiki/Grande_Epreuve" class="mw-redirect" title="Grande Epreuve">Grande Epreuve</a></i>, which counted toward the <a href="/wiki/European_Championship_(auto_racing)" class="mw-redirect" title="European Championship (auto racing)">European Championship</a> from 1935 to 1939. The 1935 event was considered to be one of the greatest motorsports victories of all time. Italian legend <a href="/wiki/Tazio_Nuvolari" title="Tazio Nuvolari">Tazio Nuvolari</a>, driving a hopelessly outdated and underpowered Alfa Romeo against state-of-the-art Mercedes and Auto Unions drove a very hard race in appalling conditions. After a dreadful start, he was able to pass a number of cars, particularly while some of the German cars pitted. But after a botched pit stop that cost him six minutes, he drove on the limit, made up that time and was second by the start of the last lap; 35 seconds behind leader <a href="/wiki/Manfred_von_Brauchitsch" title="Manfred von Brauchitsch">Manfred von Brauchitsch</a> in a Mercedes. Von Brauchitsch had ruined his tyres by pushing very hard in the dreadful conditions and Nuvolari was able to catch the German and take victory in front of the stunned German High Command and 350,000 spectators. The small 42-year-old Italian ended up finishing in front of eight running Silver Arrows. Second placed <a href="/wiki/Hans_Stuck" title="Hans Stuck">Hans Stuck</a> was two minutes behind Nuvolari.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A,_N%C3%BCrburgring,_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A%2C_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A%2C_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A%2C_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A%2C_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A%2C_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A%2C_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1989-015-36A,_N%C3%BCrburgring,_Bernd_Rosemeyer_in_Rennwagen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bernd_Rosemeyer" title="Bernd Rosemeyer">Bernd Rosemeyer</a> racing an <a href="/wiki/Auto_Union_racing_car" title="Auto Union racing car">Auto Union Typ C</a> in <a href="/wiki/1937_German_Grand_Prix" title="1937 German Grand Prix">1937</a>, he finished third.</div></div></div>
<p>The 1936 race was won by German driver <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Rosemeyer" title="Bernd Rosemeyer">Bernd Rosemeyer</a>, driving an Auto Union, who also won the Eifelrennen event at the Nordschleife in spectacular style, earning the nickname "Fog Master". The 1937 race saw Carraciola win again in a Mercedes and Auto Union driver <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ernst_Von_Delius&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ernst Von Delius (page does not exist)">Ernst Von Delius</a> die after a crash near the Antonius Bridge on the main straight. Von Delius hit the back of Briton <a href="/wiki/Richard_Seaman" title="Richard Seaman">Richard Seaman</a>'s Mercedes at 250&#160;km/h (154&#160;mph) and he went flying through a bush and into a field. The car then ended up as a wreck on the side of the road next to the main straight. Von Delius suffered a broken leg and other injuries. He was expected to make a full recovery; but died the following night of thrombosis and other complications. Seaman crashed into a kilometre post and suffered minor injuries; but he survived.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The Briton would come back to the Nürburgring to win the 1938 race, also in a Mercedes, which was to be his only championship Grand Prix victory. In 1939 a new track was built near <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, called the <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deutschlandring&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Deutschlandring (page does not exist)">Deutschlandring</a></i>, which was intended to host the 1940 German Grand Prix. However, because of the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the race was never run and the circuit never utilised for competition. In the same year, Caracciola took his sixth German Grand Prix victory. Soon afterwards Germany was at war and the Grand Prix did not return to international status until 1951.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="N.C3.BCrburgring_and_the_World_Championship"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nürburgring_and_the_World_Championship">Nürburgring and the World Championship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nürburgring and the World Championship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring,_Juan_Manuel_Fangio,_Mercedes_(3)x.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Juan_Manuel_Fangio%2C_Mercedes_%283%29x.JPG/220px-Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Juan_Manuel_Fangio%2C_Mercedes_%283%29x.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Juan_Manuel_Fangio%2C_Mercedes_%283%29x.JPG/330px-Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Juan_Manuel_Fangio%2C_Mercedes_%283%29x.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Juan_Manuel_Fangio%2C_Mercedes_%283%29x.JPG/440px-Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring%2C_Juan_Manuel_Fangio%2C_Mercedes_%283%29x.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gro%C3%9Fer_Preis_von_Europa_-1954_N%C3%BCrburgring,_Juan_Manuel_Fangio,_Mercedes_(3)x.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_Fangio" title="Juan Manuel Fangio">Juan Manuel Fangio</a> won the <a href="/wiki/European_Grand_Prix" title="European Grand Prix">European Grand Prix</a> at the <i>Ring</i> in <a href="/wiki/1954_German_Grand_Prix" title="1954 German Grand Prix">1954</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_W196" title="Mercedes-Benz W196">Mercedes-Benz W196</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Germany had been separated into Eastern and Western territories, and West Germany was banned from international sporting events until 1951. A non-championship Formula 2 race was won by <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Ascari" title="Alberto Ascari">Alberto Ascari</a> in 1950 at the Nürburgring.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The German Grand Prix was included as part of the new Formula One championship in its second season. The Nordschleife was to be the mainstay of West Germany's premier motor racing event for the next quarter of a century, and was to be known as the toughest and most technically challenging circuit on the F1 calendar. The expanse of the circuit allowed an average of 375,000 spectators each year to watch the very popular event.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The 1951 race was one where Argentine <a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_Fangio" title="Juan Manuel Fangio">Juan Manuel Fangio</a> led for 14 laps; but he had to refuel his Alfa and he only had third and fourth gears left. While in the pits, he was overtaken by Ascari in a Ferrari and went on to finish second behind the Italian. Fangio won for the first time in 1954 in a Mercedes; the first time a factory Mercedes Grand Prix car had been taken part in 15 years. He won driving the <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_W196" title="Mercedes-Benz W196">new open-wheeled W196</a>, built at Fangio's request. This event also saw the death of Argentine driver <a href="/wiki/Onofre_Marim%C3%B3n" title="Onofre Marimón">Onofre Marimón</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Maserati_250F" title="Maserati 250F">Maserati 250F</a> during practice. Due to lack of knowledge of the circuit, crucial to doing well at the Nürburgring, he failed to negotiate a tricky bend near the Adenauer Bridge. He went off the road, ploughed through a hedge and down a steep slope, and the car sheared off a tree and tumbled down the slope. Once it came to a stop, Marimón was pinned underneath the 670&#160;kg (1,480&#160;lb) 250F; cars in those days had no roll-over bars to protect the driver from the car crushing its driver; which is what happened to Marimón. The hapless Argentine's injuries were bad enough to kill him a few minutes after the incident. His compatriot Fangio went to check the wreckage of the Maserati; he found it in fourth gear of four; this corner was normally taken in an F1 car in third gear. This proved the accident to be unfortunately of driver error. The 1955 event was cancelled in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/1955_Le_Mans_disaster" title="1955 Le Mans disaster">Le Mans disaster</a>; all auto racing in Germany (and much of Europe) was banned until the tracks could be upgraded. Fangio would win the next two events.
</p><p>The 1957 event saw a number of changes. It included a Formula 2 race which was run concurrently alongside the Formula One cars. The track had been resurfaced and the concrete road surface (which was in very bad shape) which made up the pit straight, the Sudkurve and the straight behind the pits was taken out and replaced with tarmac. The 1957 event is, like Nuvolari's 1935 victory, one of the greatest motorsports victories of all time. Fangio led for the beginning of the race in front of two Ferraris driven by Britons <a href="/wiki/Mike_Hawthorn" title="Mike Hawthorn">Mike Hawthorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Collins_(racing_driver)" title="Peter Collins (racing driver)">Peter Collins</a>. Fangio planned to refuel during mid-distance; and he did. The pit stop was expected to take 30 seconds. It was a botched one and it took 78 seconds. Fangio was now nearly a minute behind Hawthorn and Collins. He began a charge where he made up several seconds on each lap. He tore huge chunks out of the lap record, breaking it nine times. On the 21st lap (the second-to-last lap) he passed Collins behind the pits, then Hawthorn late into the same lap. The 46-year-old Argentine won the race (his 24th and final F1 victory) and his fifth and final championship. 1958 saw the distance shortened to 18 laps; Briton Tony Brooks won. Collins crashed into a ditch next to the track at Pflanzgarten, and was thrown out of his car and hit a tree head first. He received severe head injuries and eventually died in a hospital near the circuit.
</p><p>1959 saw the race go to the ultra-fast AVUS circuit in Berlin. This was the only Formula One race that took place there and was won by Brooks in a Ferrari. The AVUS circuit was now made up of two 2.5-mile straights, a tight left-handed hairpin at one end and a huge 43° brick banking constructed in 1937 on the other end, which was known as <i>Die Mauer des Todes</i> (English: "The Wall of Death").<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The straights could only go as far as 2.5 miles because if they went any further, the circuit would cross into <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>. Frenchman and prominent Formula One driver <a href="/wiki/Jean_Behra" title="Jean Behra">Jean Behra</a> was killed during a support sportscar race driving a Porsche. He lost control and the Porsche went up and flew off the banking there, which had no safety wall or barrier of any kind. Behra was thrown 300 feet from his car and his head struck a flagpole; killing him instantly. Behra had been fired by Ferrari after an altercation in a restaurant with the Scuderia's manager shortly before his death.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1962-08-05_Graham_Hill,_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_(sw).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/1962-08-05_Graham_Hill%2C_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_%28sw%29.jpg/220px-1962-08-05_Graham_Hill%2C_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_%28sw%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/1962-08-05_Graham_Hill%2C_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_%28sw%29.jpg/330px-1962-08-05_Graham_Hill%2C_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_%28sw%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/1962-08-05_Graham_Hill%2C_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_%28sw%29.jpg/440px-1962-08-05_Graham_Hill%2C_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_%28sw%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2362" data-file-height="1577" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1962-08-05_Graham_Hill,_BRM_-_Hatzenbach_(sw).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/1962_German_Grand_Prix" title="1962 German Grand Prix">1962</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graham_Hill" title="Graham Hill">Graham Hill</a> won the race on his way to the World Championship.</div></div></div>
<p>1960 was a one-off Formula 2 race held on the smaller 4.7-mile (7.7&#160;km) <i>Sudschleife</i> (South Loop) section of the Nürburgring. This race was given the title German Grand Prix because the German national car club (ADAC), the organizers of the race, had made the decision to run the race as a Formula 2 race at the Sudschleife because of complaints by some of the drivers (even by Stirling Moss, a champion of a certain amount of danger in motor racing) of the extreme danger, speed and even blandness of the AVUS track- which, as described above, had a very uncomplicated layout. In an indication of the rather cavalier attitude towards safety in motor racing in those days, the drivers felt the risk of AVUS was not worth it, they then refused to race there and the Formula 1 race was cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the rest of the 1960s saw nine Formula One events take place at the Nordschleife. The 1961 event was won by Briton <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Moss" title="Stirling Moss">Stirling Moss</a> driving a privately entered <a href="/wiki/Team_Lotus" title="Team Lotus">Lotus</a>. Moss was able to hold off the two powerful and faster Ferraris of American <a href="/wiki/Phil_Hill" title="Phil Hill">Phil Hill</a> and German <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Von_Trips" class="mw-redirect" title="Wolfgang Von Trips">Wolfgang Von Trips</a>. A clever tyre choice and skilful driving in wet weather conditions helped Moss to finish 16 seconds in front of Von Trips. The 1964 event saw Dutch gentleman driver <a href="/wiki/Carel_Godin_de_Beaufort" title="Carel Godin de Beaufort">Carel Godin de Beaufort</a> die during practice after he went off at Bergwerk corner. His orange Porsche went through bushes, down an embankment and then hit a tree. He died from his injuries in a hospital near the circuit. Briton <a href="/wiki/John_Surtees" title="John Surtees">John Surtees</a> won for the second year in a row from <a href="/wiki/Jim_Clark" title="Jim Clark">Jim Clark</a>. In 1965 Clark won, his seventh Formula One victory of that season and won his second Drivers' Championship in a Lotus. 1966 saw changeable weather conditions and a battle between Australian <a href="/wiki/Jack_Brabham" title="Jack Brabham">Jack Brabham</a> and Surtees with Brabham taking victory. Briton <a href="/wiki/John_Taylor_(racing_driver)" title="John Taylor (racing driver)">John Taylor</a> was killed after he hit the back of Belgian <a href="/wiki/Jacky_Ickx" title="Jacky Ickx">Jacky Ickx</a>'s Formula 2 <a href="/wiki/Matra_MS5" title="Matra MS5">Matra MS5</a> near the bridge at Quddlebacher and Flugplatz. Taylor crashed and his Brabham BT11 caught fire. He received severe burns, from which he succumbed to a month later.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stewart,_J.,_Matra_MS10,_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Stewart%2C_J.%2C_Matra_MS10%2C_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg/220px-Stewart%2C_J.%2C_Matra_MS10%2C_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Stewart%2C_J.%2C_Matra_MS10%2C_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg/330px-Stewart%2C_J.%2C_Matra_MS10%2C_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Stewart%2C_J.%2C_Matra_MS10%2C_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg/440px-Stewart%2C_J.%2C_Matra_MS10%2C_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2126" data-file-height="1407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stewart,_J.,_Matra_MS10,_2._Rd_1968-08-04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jackie_Stewart" title="Jackie Stewart">Jackie Stewart</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Matra_MS10" title="Matra MS10">Matra MS10</a> on his way to a dominant victory in <a href="/wiki/1968_German_Grand_Prix" title="1968 German Grand Prix">1968</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1967 a chicane was added before the pits but the cars were already matching 1965 lap times. The 1968 event was yet the scene of another great victory. This event took place in heavy rain and fog. Briton <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Stewart" title="Jackie Stewart">Jackie Stewart</a> won the race by more than four minutes from <a href="/wiki/Graham_Hill" title="Graham Hill">Graham Hill</a>; he was 30 seconds ahead of the second placed Hill by the end of the first lap. Stewart held the lead amid a driving rainstorm and thick fog.
</p><p>Jacky Ickx won in 1969 driving a Brabham. The Belgian had made a bad start, clawed back through the field and after a long battle with Stewart, Ickx took the lead from Stewart on Lap 5. The Scot fell back with gearbox problems, leaving the Belgian in a dominant position. Stewart was able to hold on to second place. German driver <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Mitter" title="Gerhard Mitter">Gerhard Mitter</a> was killed during practice driving a <a href="/w/index.php?title=BMW_269&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="BMW 269 (page does not exist)">BMW 269</a> Formula 2 car after his rear suspension failed and the car went straight on at the downhill section near the very fast Schwedenkreuz bend. This was the fifth Formula One-related fatality at the 14.2-mile German circuit in 15 years, the most out of all the circuits yet used for the championship.
</p><p>1970 however was to start the demise of the Nordschleife for international motor racing. After the death of <a href="/wiki/Piers_Courage" title="Piers Courage">Piers Courage</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1970_Dutch_Grand_Prix" title="1970 Dutch Grand Prix">Dutch Grand Prix</a> a few months previously, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_Drivers%27_Association" title="Grand Prix Drivers&#39; Association">Grand Prix Drivers' Association</a> had a meeting at a hotel in London. Despite considerable pressure from outside parties they voted not to race at the notorious German circuit unless significant changes were made to the track safety conditions of the Nürburgring. Speeds of Formula One cars had increased dramatically as had the technology- the cars were lapping the 'Ring more than 2 minutes faster than they had in 1951, and it had become clear that the Nürburgring, which was essentially a rough, unprotected road that went through forests and valleys situated in an expansive mountain range, was too dangerous and outdated for Grand Prix racing. The changes demanded by the drivers were refused by the circuit owners and organizers and could not possibly be made in time for the 1970 event, forcing a hasty switch to the fast Hockenheimring, which had already been upgraded with safety features. The race itself proved to be an exciting one, as it was won by Austrian <a href="/wiki/Jochen_Rindt" title="Jochen Rindt">Jochen Rindt</a>, resisting a charging Ickx in a Ferrari.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi,_Lotus_72_(kl).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi%2C_Lotus_72_%28kl%29.JPG/220px-1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi%2C_Lotus_72_%28kl%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi%2C_Lotus_72_%28kl%29.JPG/330px-1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi%2C_Lotus_72_%28kl%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi%2C_Lotus_72_%28kl%29.JPG/440px-1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi%2C_Lotus_72_%28kl%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1217" data-file-height="810" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1971_Emerson_Fittipaldi,_Lotus_72_(kl).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emerson_Fittipaldi" title="Emerson Fittipaldi">Emerson Fittipaldi</a> in <a href="/wiki/1971_German_Grand_Prix" title="1971 German Grand Prix">1971</a>, the year the race returned to the <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>1971 saw the race return to a modified Nürburgring. It was made smoother, straighter and was fitted with Armco barriers and run-off areas wherever possible. But with the layout being virtually the same as before, the circuit retained much of the character that led Stewart to call it "The Green Hell." It was less dangerous than it had been before, but the 'Ring was still by far the most technically challenging circuit on the F1 calendar. It was still dangerously rough and narrow in many areas, and even though some of the worst bumps, jumps and windy straights (particularly at Brunnchen and the Adenauer Bridge) had been smoothed over or made straight, there were still some big jumps on the track, particularly at Flugplatz and Pflanzgarten.  Also, there were still some parts of the track that did not have Armco, but more of this was added through the years. The first event on the rebuilt Nordschleife saw Stewart win from his teammate <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Cevert" title="François Cevert">François Cevert</a>, who battled the Swiss <a href="/wiki/Clay_Regazzoni" title="Clay Regazzoni">Clay Regazzoni</a> for 2nd with for nearly a quarter of the race. The 1972 event saw Jacky Ickx dominate in his Ferrari and Stewart crashed on the last lap after tangling with Regazzoni. The 1973 race was dominated by Tyrrell teammates Stewart and Cevert; and it was to be the 27th and last victory of Stewart's illustrious career. 1974 saw New Zealander <a href="/wiki/Howden_Ganley" title="Howden Ganley">Howden Ganley</a> crash heavily at Hatzenbach, seriously injuring the Kiwi. Ganley had already crashed heavily at the Nürburgring the year before and he decided to end his F1 career after his 1974 accident. The race saw Regazzoni win after Austrian <a href="/wiki/Niki_Lauda" title="Niki Lauda">Niki Lauda</a> (who had crashed and had broken his wrist at the previous year's German Grand Prix) and South African <a href="/wiki/Jody_Scheckter" title="Jody Scheckter">Jody Scheckter</a> tangled on the first lap; Lauda was out but Scheckter went on to finish second. Briton and multiple motorcycle world champion <a href="/wiki/Mike_Hailwood" title="Mike Hailwood">Mike Hailwood</a> crashed heavily in a McLaren at Pflanzgarten and broke his leg, his auto racing career was effectively ended by this crash. 1975 saw Lauda become the only driver ever to lap the old Nürburgring in under seven minutes; the Austrian lapped the monstrous circuit in his Ferrari in 6 minutes, 58.6 seconds at an average speed of 122&#160;mph (196&#160;km/h), which was good enough for pole position. But like so many years gone by, the weekend saw yet another serious accident. Briton <a href="/wiki/Ian_Ashley" title="Ian Ashley">Ian Ashley</a> crashed his <a href="/wiki/Williams_FW" title="Williams FW">Williams FW</a> during practice at Pflanzgarten and he was seriously injured; he did not race in Formula One again for at least two years. Argentine <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Reutemann" title="Carlos Reutemann">Carlos Reutemann</a> took victory after keeping the lead for five remaining laps while Lauda had a puncture after leading for the first nine laps. Briton <a href="/wiki/Tom_Pryce" title="Tom Pryce">Tom Pryce</a> ran as high as second after starting 17th in an under-funded Shadow, but he finished fourth after very hot fuel began to leak into his cockpit. Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Laffite" title="Jacques Laffite">Jacques Laffite</a> and Lauda passed Pryce. Laffite finished second which was a milestone for Briton <a href="/wiki/Frank_Williams_(Formula_One)" title="Frank Williams (Formula One)">Frank Williams's</a> struggling team; it was the English Williams's first real taste of success in Formula One. Pryce received a medal for his efforts. The 1975 Grand Prix was the fastest race ever run on the old Nürburgring; Lauda's teammate Clay Regazzoni posted the fastest lap at 7:06.4- which was to be the lap record of the old circuit.
</p><p>Over the years, the Nürburgring was modified several times at the behest of the drivers. However, the 1976 event was one that was to go down in history. Lauda, the reigning world champion, was dissatisfied with the safety arrangements of the mammoth circuit and attempted to organise a boycott the race during a meeting at the third race of the season in <a href="/wiki/1976_United_States_Grand_Prix_West" title="1976 United States Grand Prix West">Long Beach, California</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Formula One in the 1970s was the beginning towards a safer kind of motor racing and the Nürburgring was considered to be something of an anachronism at that time. However, by its very nature, the Nürburgring was almost impossible to be made safe in its old configuration. It did not have enough marshals and medical support to ensure the circuit's safety- it needed five to six times the marshals and medical staff that a typical F1 race needed at the time, but <a href="/wiki/Huschke_von_Hanstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Huschke von Hanstein">Huschke von Hanstein</a> and the German organizers were unwilling and possibly unable to provide them- it was extremely expensive; and the spectators viewing the race in the countryside would get into the track for free. In addition to the considerable expense of providing adequate support to the drivers, its geography made the modifications demanded by both the drivers and FIA also prohibitively expensive. There were several parts that were nearly inaccessible to the marshals- there were a number of places where run-off areas could not be built because they were not flat enough, there were parts that were too narrow because there was a cliff face on one side and a drop-off on another, etc. However, the Nürburgring's organizers had a three-year contract with Formula One starting with the 1974 race which included making the track safer. Lauda was outvoted by other drivers because most of them felt that they should complete the contract so as to avoid any legal difficulties; the 1976 race was the last race on that contract. Although the contract included making the circuit safer over those years (and the organizers did that) it had already been decided that the 1976 race would be the last race at the Nordschleife. In addition to safety issues, the increasing commercialisation of Formula One was a factor as well. The extraordinary length of the Nordschleife made it all but impossible for any broadcasting organisation to effectively cover a race there.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg/220px-LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg/330px-LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg/440px-LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3396" data-file-height="2260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LaudaNiki19760731Ferrari312T2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Niki_Lauda" title="Niki Lauda">Niki Lauda</a>, pictured here during practice, crashed at the <a href="/wiki/1976_German_Grand_Prix" title="1976 German Grand Prix">1976 race</a>, suffering life-threatening burns.</div></div></div>
<p>As the 1976 race started parts of the circuit were wet and overcast, and other parts were dry and bathed in sunlight, another classic problem of the Nürburgring. After pitting to change from wet to dry tyres at the end of the first lap, Lauda came out again, far behind the leader, West German <a href="/wiki/Jochen_Mass" title="Jochen Mass">Jochen Mass</a>. While pushing hard to make up time on the second lap, Lauda crashed at the fast left hand kink before Bergwerk corner over six miles (10.8&#160;km) into the lap, one of the more inaccessible parts of the circuit. Going through the corner, Lauda lost control of his Ferrari when its rear suspension failed. The car crashed into a grass embankment and burst into flames. During the impact, Lauda's helmet was wrenched from his head, and his burning Ferrari was hit by the cars of <a href="/wiki/Brett_Lunger" title="Brett Lunger">Brett Lunger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arturo_Merzario" title="Arturo Merzario">Arturo Merzario</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harald_Ertl" title="Harald Ertl">Harald Ertl</a>. Lunger pulled Lauda out of the burning wreckage instead of the ill-equipped track marshals, who only arrived at the scene well after the impact. The resilient Austrian was standing and talking to other drivers right after the accident and his injuries were initially not expected to be serious. However, he had been severely burned and had been breathing in toxic fumes, which damaged his circulatory system. It took the only medical helicopter at the circuit 6 minutes to reach the accident scene from the pits area, as opposed to at most a minute at any other circuit on the Formula One calendar. Lauda later lapsed into a coma and nearly died, putting him out of action for six weeks. The event was red-flagged and restarted; long-time Grand Prix driver <a href="/wiki/Chris_Amon" title="Chris Amon">Chris Amon</a> elected not to take the restart. This was the last Grand Prix the unlucky New Zealander drove in. Englishman <a href="/wiki/James_Hunt" title="James Hunt">James Hunt</a> won this race, which turned out to be crucial for his championship chances that year. After 49 years of hosting the German Grand Prix the old Nürburgring never hosted a Grand Prix again, and the race returned to Hockenheim.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hockenheim">Hockenheim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hockenheim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The fast, flat Hockenheim circuit near Heidelberg almost played sole host to the German Grand Prix for the next 30 years. The 1977 event was won by Lauda, but was also notable when local driver <a href="/wiki/Hans_Heyer" title="Hans Heyer">Hans Heyer</a> competed in the race despite failing to qualify. The 1979 event was one where Swiss <a href="/wiki/Clay_Regazzoni" title="Clay Regazzoni">Clay Regazzoni</a> in his Williams attempted to chase down his teammate, Australian <a href="/wiki/Alan_Jones_(racing_driver)" title="Alan Jones (racing driver)">Alan Jones</a>, but to no avail. 1980 was won by Frenchman Jacques Laffite in a Ligier, but it was overshadowed by Patrick Depailler's fatal accident at the Ostkurve (which in those days, had no chicane and was flat-out) in testing for Alfa Romeo a few days before the race weekend. The 1981 event saw a tremendous battle between Jones and rising star <a href="/wiki/Alain_Prost" title="Alain Prost">Alain Prost</a> in a Renault, with Jones passing Prost in the stadium due to interference by Prost's backmarker teammate Rene Arnoux. The race was won by Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Piquet" title="Nelson Piquet">Nelson Piquet</a> after Jones pitted with problems with his Williams. The 1982 race saw changes to the circuit; most notably a chicane to the ultra-fast Ost-Kurve. It also saw the end of Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Didier_Pironi" title="Didier Pironi">Didier Pironi</a>'s career; he had an appalling crash in the pouring rain during qualifying. After he hit the back of Prost's Renault, Pironi was launched skyward and then rolled for some time until coming to a stop. Pironi, who was leading the championship at the time, had such serious leg injuries that FIA doctor <a href="/wiki/Sid_Watkins" title="Sid Watkins">Sid Watkins</a> nearly had to amputate Pironi's legs in order to get him out of the wrecked Ferrari. The way Formula One cars were designed at the time was in such a way that the drivers sat so far forward in the cockpit that their legs and feet were way in front of the front axle, leaving those human body parts dangerously exposed. They were only protected by only the chassis structure and the aluminium bodywork. During the race, Piquet physically attacked Chilean driver <a href="/wiki/Eliseo_Salazar" title="Eliseo Salazar">Eliseo Salazar</a> after Salazar punted off the irate Brazilian at the new Ostkurve chicane while leading the race. <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Tambay" title="Patrick Tambay">Patrick Tambay</a> won his first race for Ferrari. The 1984 race saw Prost (now driving a McLaren) win and Toleman rookie <a href="/wiki/Ayrton_Senna" title="Ayrton Senna">Ayrton Senna</a> drive very hard at the front of the field during the beginning of the race.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg/250px-Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg/375px-Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg/500px-Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="453" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alboreto_1985-08-02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Michele_Alboreto" title="Michele Alboreto">Michele Alboreto</a> took victory at the one-off return to the new Nürburgring in <a href="/wiki/1985_German_Grand_Prix" title="1985 German Grand Prix">1985</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>1985 saw a one-off return to the new 2.8-mile (4.5&#160;km) Nürburgring Grand Prix circuit, which had been built upon the site of the old Nordschliefe and hosted the <a href="/wiki/European_Grand_Prix" title="European Grand Prix">European Grand Prix</a> the year before. It was a race where a number of drivers battled for the lead; it was won by Italian <a href="/wiki/Michele_Alboreto" title="Michele Alboreto">Michele Alboreto</a>.
</p><p>The international motorsports governing body at the time, FISA, had instituted a policy of long-term contracts for one circuit per Grand Prix. The choice for the German Grand Prix was between the new Nürburgring and Hockenheim. It was the latter that was chosen and it stayed there for the next 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The 1986 event was one where a number of the leaders ran on fumes at the very end; top three drivers Piquet, Senna and Prost were all running out of fuel and although Brazilians Piquet and Senna finished 1–2, Prost finished sixth after his car was completely drained of fuel. The 1988 race was run in wet conditions; these conditions were particularly treacherous at Hockenheim because the circuit ran through a forest and the thick moisture from the rain tended to hang in the air because of the trees that surrounded the track. Even when it was not raining, the track still did not dry. Senna (now driving a McLaren) took advantage of his skill in the wet to win over his teammate Prost. The 1989 race was near the height Prost and Senna's famous rivalry and this race was one where the two McLaren teammates drove on their absolute limits throughout the entire race. Prost's gearbox malfunctioned and lost use of sixth gear on the second to last lap and Senna passed him to take the victory. Senna won the next year's race as well from Italian <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Nannini" title="Alessandro Nannini">Alessandro Nannini</a>. The 1992 race saw further changes to the Ostkurve after a crash that <a href="/wiki/%C3%89rik_Comas" title="Érik Comas">Érik Comas</a> had there in 1991; it was turned into a more complex chicane rather than simple left-right chicane with a tyre wall in the middle. 1994 saw a further change to the third chicane to make it slower in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/1994_San_Marino_Grand_Prix" title="1994 San Marino Grand Prix">1994 San Marino Grand Prix</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg/220px-Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg/330px-Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg/440px-Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3270" data-file-height="2132" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Michael_Schumacher_1997.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The arrival of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Schumacher" title="Michael Schumacher">Michael Schumacher</a> (pictured here in <a href="/wiki/1997_German_Grand_Prix" title="1997 German Grand Prix">1997</a>) at the top of Grand Prix racing saw a rise in popularity for the sport in Germany. He would win the race four times.</div></div></div>
<p>1995 saw German <a href="/wiki/Michael_Schumacher" title="Michael Schumacher">Michael Schumacher</a> win, he was the first German to win his home race since Rudolf Caracciola in 1939. Formula One interest in Germany had peaked during the emergence of Schumacher. 1997 saw an exceptional win by Austrian <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Berger" title="Gerhard Berger">Gerhard Berger</a>. But the 2000 race was to play host to a number of problems. During the race, a disgruntled ex-Mercedes employee went onto the circuit during the race and disrupted the proceedings; and Jean Alesi had a huge accident at the third chicane and suffered dizziness for three days. And on the far side of the circuit (where the Ost-Kurve was) it was dry, but in the stadium section and the pits, it was pouring with rain. Rubens Barrichello won the race from 18th on the grid, which was his first Formula One victory. 2001 saw a huge accident at the start between Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Luciano_Burti" title="Luciano Burti">Luciano Burti</a> in a Prost and Schumacher in a Ferrari; this race was won by Michael's brother Ralf in a BMW-powered Williams.
</p><p>2002 saw the Hockenheimring dramatically shortened and the layout altered. The forest straights were removed and more corners were added to increase the technical challenge of the circuit. The circuit went from 4.2 to 2.7 miles long. Michael Schumacher won in that year. 2003 was the year Colombian <a href="/wiki/Juan_Pablo_Montoya" title="Juan Pablo Montoya">Juan Pablo Montoya</a> won for Williams-BMW, it was the second German GP victory in three years for engines of the Bavarian car maker. That year also saw the last appearance of the British <a href="/wiki/Arrows_Grand_Prix_International" title="Arrows Grand Prix International">Arrows</a> team, who had been involved in Formula One since 1978. 2004 saw Schumacher continue his domination of that season by winning the German Grand Prix and Spaniard <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Alonso" title="Fernando Alonso">Fernando Alonso</a> won the following year in a Renault after his main rival <a href="/wiki/Kimi_R%C3%A4ikk%C3%B6nen" title="Kimi Räikkönen">Kimi Räikkönen</a> suffered a hydraulics failure and retired. 2006 saw Renault's experimental mass damper system deemed legal by the race stewards but it was banned by the FIA. Renault did not use the system for the race and it proved to be their downfall as Schumacher won his home race in a Ferrari.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternating_venues">Alternating venues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Alternating venues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2006 it was announced that from 2007 until 2010, the German Grand Prix would be shared between the Nürburgring GP circuit (former home of the European and Luxembourg Grands Prix) and the Hockenheimring. The former would hold the races in 2007 and 2009 and the latter in 2008 and 2010. However, the name for the 2007 Grand Prix was later changed. While it was originally intended to be the German Grand Prix,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> owing to a dispute with <a href="/wiki/Hockenheimring" title="Hockenheimring">Hockenheim</a> over the naming rights of the race, the race was eventually held under the title "Großer Preis von Europa" (<a href="/wiki/European_Grand_Prix" title="European Grand Prix">European Grand Prix</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> By 2009, the circuits appeared to have resolved their disputes as the Nürburgring race was held under the German Grand Prix title.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg/220px-Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg/330px-Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg/440px-Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4977" data-file-height="3318" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fernando_Alonso_German_GP_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fernando_Alonso" title="Fernando Alonso">Fernando Alonso</a> won in <a href="/wiki/2010_German_Grand_Prix" title="2010 German Grand Prix">2010</a> under controversial circumstances.</div></div></div>
<p>The 2010 GP, held in Hockenheim, at one stage appeared to be in jeopardy as the track owners, the city and the state of <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a>, were not willing any more to lose money due to the high licensing costs imposed by F1 management. In addition, talks with <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Ecclestone" title="Bernie Ecclestone">Bernie Ecclestone</a> were hampered by his Hitler quotes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (July 2016)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> If the track had been relieved from being the venue, the owners were intending to returning the track back to its former layout.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2009)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, on 30 September 2009, it was announced that the circuit had agreed a deal which would keep it on the calendar until 2018, under a new deal which saw the circuit management and FOA sharing the financial burden of hosting the event.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This alternating pattern has continued, with Hockenheim hosting the race in even years, and the Nürburgring hosting the race in odd years until 2013.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biennial_race_at_Hockenheim">Biennial race at Hockenheim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biennial race at Hockenheim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Nürburgring went through a change of ownership during 2014, but the new owners were unable to sign an agreement to continue hosting the race in odd-numbered years.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-No_German_GP_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-No_German_GP-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The Hockenheimring wasn't able to host the 2015 or 2017 events either and the German Grand Prix was not run.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Thus the race became a biennial event, returning to the calendar in 2016 and 2018 at Hockenheim.<sup id="cite_ref-biannual_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biannual-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="One_more_visit_to_Hockenheim">One more visit to Hockenheim</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: One more visit to Hockenheim">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The deal with Hockenheim to host the German Grand Prix concluded following the 2018 event.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the future of the event was put in doubt.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> However, a deal was reached in August 2018 to hold one more event at Hockenheim in <a href="/wiki/2019_Formula_One_World_Championship" title="2019 Formula One World Championship">2019</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hockenheim_2019_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hockenheim_2019-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> While the German Grand Prix is not on the Formula One calendar in 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> a race titled <a href="/wiki/Eifel_Grand_Prix" class="mw-redirect" title="Eifel Grand Prix">Eifel Grand Prix</a> was at the <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> in October.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_names_and_sponsors">Official names and sponsors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Official names and sponsors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>1951–1953, 1956–1959, 1951–1953, 1962–1967, 1969–1973, 1975–1981, 1986: Grosser Preis von Deutschland</li>
<li>1954, 1961, 1968, 1974: Grosser Preis von Europa</li>
<li>1982–1983: <a href="/wiki/Automobilclub_von_Deutschland" title="Automobilclub von Deutschland">AvD</a>-Grosser Preis von Deutschland</li>
<li>1984–1985: <a href="/wiki/Automobilclub_von_Deutschland" title="Automobilclub von Deutschland">AvD</a>-Großer Preis von Deutschland</li>
<li>1987: <a href="/wiki/Mobil_1" title="Mobil 1">Mobil</a> Grosser Preis von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1988: <a href="/wiki/Mobil_1" title="Mobil 1">Mobil 1</a> Grosser Preis von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1989–1997, 1999–2006: Grosser <a href="/wiki/Mobil_1" title="Mobil 1">Mobil 1</a> Preis von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1998: Großer <a href="/wiki/Mobil_1" title="Mobil 1">Mobil 1</a> Preis von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2008–2014: Grosser Preis <a href="/wiki/Santander_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Santander Group">Santander</a> von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2016: Grosser Preis von Deutschland (no official sponsor)<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2018: <a href="/wiki/Emirates_(airline)" title="Emirates (airline)">Emirates</a> Grosser Preis von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>2019: <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> Grosser Preis von Deutschland<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hockenheim_2019_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hockenheim_2019-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Winners_of_the_German_Grand_Prix">Winners of the German Grand Prix</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_Grand_Prix&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Winners of the German Grand Prix">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<i>Both non-Formula One World Championship events post World War II were run to Formula 2 regulations.<br /></i>
